Abstract 
This master’s thesis investigates the issues of construction-related accidents on construction 
sites, by investigating a concept, which involves the implementation of location-based 
sensors, more specifically, the RTK-GNSS system, to be worn by workers and installed on 
vehicles in order to generate useful safety related data, that can support safety managers in 
their safety-related decisions. 
This thesis also investigates the possible complications that could occur with the 
implementation of location-based technology on construction sites in Denmark, and how 
these could be handled. 
The thesis also investigates the stances and opinions of several Danish companies involved 
in the construction industry in Denmark, regarding the implementation of this concept, 
through interviews.  
The general opinion of the Danish companies about the implementation of the concept was 
mostly positive. It was clear that the concept had the potential to generate useful data, that 
could aid safety managers in reducing risks and accidents and improving safety on 
construction sites. Although there were some key concerns towards the implementation of 
location-based technologies. Most of the interviewed companies had some concerns about 
the data gathering aspect of the concept. Workers might not feel comfortable with being 
monitored, additionally the concept must also comply with the general data protection 
regulations (GDPR). Other complications were the cost and physical setup of the 
technologies. 
Some of these complications can be overcome by making the generated data anonymous 
and by using the tool to investigate patterns instead of individual workers. 
Furthermore, two prototype algorithms have been developed as tools to read the generated 
data from the RTK-GNSS systems, and very easily show the results that are the user is 
investigating for, without having to manually read through all the generated data.

Page 7 of 246 
 
Introduction  
The construction industry is one of the largest industrial sectors and therefore employs a 
large portion of labor globally. Over thousands of workers are injured on construction sites 
yearly. The construction industry continues to remain one of the most hazardous 
workplaces where statistics show that construction workers are 3 to 4 times more likely to 
be injured or die on the job compared to other workers [1].   
Occupational health and safety have been major concerns in the construction industry for a 
long time. Several studies have attempted to investigate the root causes of accidents on 
construction sites and how to reduce them. Some of the main causes for accidents are falls 
from heights, slipping, struck by accidents, electrocution and contact with toxins. In an 
effort to improve health and safety conditions of construction workers, legal and regulatory 
frameworks have been made to ensure safe working practices on construction sites. These 
regulations are enforced by the government agencies such as the Occupational Safety and 
Health Administration (OSHA) in the United States, the Health Safety Executive (HSE) in the 
United Kingdom and the Danish Working Environment Authority (Arbejdstilsynet) in 
Denmark.  
Despite the major efforts to improve the safety of workers on construction sites there is still 
a rise in the number of reported incidents in Denmark, with 6.360 in 2022. Since 
construction projects are different from project to project and there are changes in 
collaboration parties, it is difficult to establish a safety culture. Due to construction projects 
being highly dynamic and complex, various activities need to be coordinated to meet the 
construction schedules, which can result in construction workers frequently being exposed 
to potentially hazardous site conditions. Examples of such hazards can be poor site layouts 
that could increase the risk of collisions between vehicles and workers on foot, or heavy 
equipment operation in close proximity to workers.  
Presently the way data on construction site related accidents are collected and analyzed is 
mostly performed manually, resulting in the safety measurements being subjective and 
could vary from inspector to inspector. Another issue is the definition of near miss accidents 
and when it becomes necessary to report them. Therefore, there is a need for new methods 
for reliably gathering data for measuring safety performance objectively and with precision.  
Over the years new methods and technology has been incorporated into the construction 
industry to optimize the effectiveness of the building process. Some of the new methods 
also attempt to improve the health and safety on construction sites by integrating it in the 
design planning with BIM and other digital methods. An example of this, is the gradual 
introduction of VR, games, and 3D software as a training tool to improve the safety on 
construction sites. Although, these technologies have a long way to go, before becoming 
standard procedure in the construction industry [2]. 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
Page 8 of 246 
 
Objectives  
Technologies for automated data gathering in the construction industry have been gaining a 
lot of attention over the years. Some of these technologies, which include GNSS, RFID, UWB 
and vision-based tracking algorithms are suitable for real time location tracking and 
monitoring of resources like workers and equipment. But despite the substantial number of 
studies on the use of wearable sensors with positive results regarding the use of 
technological tools, the construction industry is slow or hesitant with adaptation of these 
technologies into everyday life. Such technologies may require a capital investment and 
acceptance from corporate decision-makers. 
This study aims to investigate a concept, that revolves around the implementation of the 
advanced location-based technology RTK-GNSS, as part of the construction sites in 
Denmark, and how the processing of the data received can be automatically interpreted 
with the development of an algorithm, as a tool to analyze close encounters and allow 
safety managers to take actions towards improving safety on construction sites. 
The study will investigate whether the concept could realistically improve the safety on 
construction sites, the possible complications that would occur with the implementation, 
and what the Danish companies involved in the construction industry’s opinions about the 
concept are. This investigation will be done primarily through literature review, case studies 
and interviews with relevant companies. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:10==
Page 9 of 246 
 
Literature review 
Overview of construction safety 
Construction site safety is more than just wearing safety equipment. It is a proactive 
framework that serves to eliminate jobsite hazards throughout the project, to promote safe 
working practices into the daily tasks and processes in order create a safe working 
environment [3]. 
The construction industry is one of the biggest industries in Denmark with over 190.435 
employees. With an industry this big, and new buildings being erected everywhere, the 
safety on construction sites is of paramount importance to consider. The following figure 
below shows the number of employees in Denmark in November 2021 distributed over 
different industries [4]. 
 
Figure 1: Distribution of employees across various industries [4] 
 
Rules and regulations in Denmark 
Danish Working Environment Authority (WEA) 
The Danish Working Environment Authority (WEA), Arbejdstilsynet in Danish, bears the 
responsibility to secure safe and healthy working environments in Denmark. The WEA issue 
guidelines and rules for the construction industry in Denmark to follow, to ensure the 
workers’ safety and correct execution of projects. Some of these important rules include the 
Danish Working Environment Act and are based on scientific knowledge and experience 
from the construction industry.  
The WEA conduct regular inspections of construction sites, to identify potential risks, and to 
ensure that they are being handled in safe ways, but also to control and to make sure that 
the employers comply with the rules that are put in place to protect the safety and health of 
the workers. The inspectors have the power to issue warnings and fines for those who do 
not comply with the safety regulations [5]. 
The WEA also work together with other organizations, including employer and worker 
organizations and unions to improve the safety on construction sites, by working together 
==PAGE:11==
Page 10 of 246 
 
on the development of new safety solutions and technologies, which makes them one of the 
biggest and relevant representatives of safety on workplaces. 
 
The Danish Building Regulations 
These regulations set out requirements for building construction in Denmark, including 
safety requirements related to fire protection, structural stability, and accessibility. They 
also require the use of certified materials and products that meet specified safety standards 
[6].  
 
Danish Standards (DS/EN) 
Danish Standards are a collection of different documents with requirements and guidelines 
related to construction processes, that ensure high quality and safety [7].  
An example could be the Danish Standard DS/EN 1090. This standard applies to the 
manufacturing and installation of structural steel and aluminum components in Denmark. It 
requires companies that manufacture or install these components to have a certified quality 
management system in place and to follow specified procedures for welding, surface 
treatment, and other processes.  
Another example could be the Danish Standard DS/EN 12811-1. This standard sets out 
requirements for temporary working platforms, including scaffolding, in the construction 
industry. It requires companies to ensure that scaffolding is designed, erected, and 
maintained in a safe and secure manner and that workers are trained in its use.  
 
International Organization for Standardization (ISO) 
ISO has several standards that apply to the construction industry, including ISO 45001, 
which sets occupational health and safety standards [8].  
Overall, these regulations and standards are designed to ensure that construction sites in 
Denmark are safe and healthy for workers and that buildings are constructed to a high 
standard of safety and quality. The Danish Working Environment Authority plays an 
important role in enforcing these regulations and can issue fines or stop work if necessary to 
ensure compliance.  
 
Statistics of accidents 
In Denmark recent data collected by WEA show that the number of reported work-related 
accidents increased from 5.550 in 2019 to 6.360 in 2022 for the construction industry [9]. 
The rise in reported work-related accidents can depend on many factors like the increase in 
employment on construction sites.  
==PAGE:12==
Page 11 of 246 
 
 
Figure 2: Reported work-related accidents yearly [9] 
According to the WEA, 1 out of 10 reported occupational accidents that are registered have 
occurred on a building site and the number has been rising for the last 10 years. The number 
of occupational accidents on a building site has increased from 6 percent in 2010 to 11 
percent in 2019. Furthermore, the report [10] indicates that the occupational accidents that 
has been reported in the main industry group of construction and civil engineering, 7 of 
these out of 10, happen on a construction site. This means that approximately 30 percent of 
work-related accidents in the construction and civil engineering industry occur somewhere 
else. An example of this could be in the warehouse or a workshop of a construction 
company, or in traffic during transportation of materials and machinery. 
Nearly 2 out of 10 occupational accidents that occur on construction sites involve individuals 
that are not employed by a company registered as a construction and civil engineering 
company. 
 
Types of accidents  
The most common types of accidents reported in the Danish construction industry are falls 
from heights, struck by accidents, physical overload/unintentional movement, and contact 
with sharp objects [11]. 
 
Figure 3: Reported accident types by the Danish Working Environment Authority [11].

==PAGE:13==
Page 12 of 246 
 
Falls 
Falls from heights is the most common occupational accident in the construction industry 
with 24 percent of the total number of occupational accidents reported to the Danish 
Working Environment Authority. Generally, falls from heights are a major public health 
problem globally with an estimated 684.000 fatal falls occurring each year and is the second 
leading cause of unintentional death injuries, after road traffic injuries [12]. 
Falls from heights often occur due to poor working conditions. When working in heights 
above 2m, safety guidelines for workers must be planned, by following the guidelines 
provided by the Danish Working Environment Authority or Branchefællesskabet for 
arbejdsmiljø i Bygge & Anlæg [13].  
Fall accidents with serious injuries or deaths in the construction industry are often due to 
falls with greater than 2m height, resulting in serious head injuries, fractured bones and 
deaths [14]. According to Eirik Albrechtsen and Stig Winge [15] workers are often falling 
through unprotected openings while working on platforms, roofs, and floors. The majority 
of these accidents occur during refurbishments when doing repair work or demolition work, 
where there might be temporary openings that workers may not be aware of.  
 
Contact with sharp objects 
Contact with sharp objects is the second most common accident type in the Danish 
construction industry with 5.045 reported incidents in the last 6 years. These types of 
accidents refer to incidents where workers sustain injuries due to cuts caused by various 
sharp objects, tools or equipment. These types of accidents can occur when using a table 
saw or miter saw, where the shield is defect, dismantled or where the blade guard is not 
lowered over the material being cut [16]. 
 
Physical overload/unintentional movement 
Physical overload and unintentional movement is the third most reported accident type. 
Lifting, carrying, and other manual handling of heavy materials and machinery is one of the 
most common causes of accidents in construction and civil engineering. Accidents can occur 
if, for example, one carries something unexpectedly heavy, loses balance, or is in a hurry. 
These accidents often result in back injuries, muscle strains, or other musculoskeletal issues. 
 
Struck by accidents 
There is reported 4.665 struck by accidents in past 5 years. These types of accidents occur 
when a worker is struck by a falling object, equipment, vehicle, or moving machinery on the 
construction site.  
 
==PAGE:14==
Page 13 of 246 
 
Human factors in construction safety 
Training and education 
Training of workers plays a significant role in developing necessary knowledge about 
appropriate safety measures while performing work on the construction site. Managers as 
the leaders on the site, must also undergo safety training. Education can help workers to 
understand the risks associated with their work and be aware of safety hazards and take 
appropriate safety measures on the job, which can include the use of safety equipment and 
tools, and how to respond to emergencies. Implementing a comprehensive training program 
can contribute to lower accident rates, and it highlights the importance of management in 
promoting safety [17]. 
 
Communication 
Good communication between workers, supervisors and management is essential for 
ensuring that proper safety policies and protocols are understood and followed. By having 
proper communication flow from top to bottom in the organization, it can help to identify 
and address safety hazards, promote a safety culture, and ensure that workers have the 
information they need to work safely [18]. 
 
Attitudes and behaviors 
Workers’ attitudes and behaviors can have an impact on their safety performance on site. 
Workers behave differently during safety training compared to their behavior on site. During 
safety training they express their willingness to follow safety procedures. However, once 
they are on site, they feel the need to overcompensate to demonstrate toughness, which 
can lead to risky behaviors and a disregard for safety procedures and they may engage in 
risky tasks to boost their self-esteem and prove their skills. In some cases, their co-workers 
encourage them. This results in experienced workers often tend to neglect safety 
procedures [18]. 
 
Existing safety measures 
Construction sites in Denmark, like in so many other countries, can be very dangerous 
workplaces, where the workers experience a lot of risks every day. Accidents and injuries 
happen due to a lot of different factors such as falls, collapsing structures, electric hazards, 
and heavy machinery. Due to this, contractors in Denmark are already implementing safety 
measures to protect their workers and reduce the number of accidents taking place. The 
following examples will show some of the safety measures that are currently being used on 
construction sites. 
 
==PAGE:15==
Page 14 of 246 
 
Risk assessment 
Before work on a construction site begins, a risk assessment is done. The Risk assessment 
identifies potential dangers and hazards. When these are identified, appropriate safety 
actions can be executed to combat these risks, either by reducing or completely eliminating 
the risks. Risk assessments are essential to protect the workers on construction sites against 
dangers and risks that can lead to accidents and deaths. 
A risk assessment can help identify potential dangers such as falling objects, unstable 
structures, dangerous chemicals, electric hazards, noise exposure and other hazardous 
materials. 
Furthermore, a risk assessment also considers environmental factors that may pose a risk to 
workers on the construction site. An example could be, if the construction site is in an area 
with extreme weather conditions, the risk assessment will highlight these potential dangers 
and appropriate safety actions can be taken to prevent accidents. 
In addition to protecting workers, risk assessments can also protect the public. Construction 
sites can become a risk to members of the public if they are not properly managed. An 
example of this could be, if a construction site is located near a busy road or pedestrian 
area, the risk assessment also considers the potential risk to the public and will identify 
measures and precautions in order to reduce these risks. 
A risk assessment can also reduce the chance of delays and disruptions on the construction 
sites. By identifying potential risks and dangers in advance, measures can be taken to 
prevent these risks from coming to fruition and ensure that the construction project stays 
on schedule and isn’t halted [19]. 
 
 
Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) 
Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) are essential to protect the workers against different 
hazards, accidents, and dangerous working conditions on construction sites, both in 
Denmark but also in a lot of other countries.  PPE includes safety helmets, safety goggles, 
ear plugs, gloves, and safety shoes where each of these equipment does a specific job. For 
example, safety helmets help protect the workers against falling objects, safety shoes 
protect against heavy materials and sharp objects, and earplugs protect against noise 
exposure. 
Employers are bound by law to provide the workers with proper protective equipment for 
their job assignments. The PPE must fit, be regularly inspected, and maintained, and the 
workers need to receive proper training on how to use them [20]. 
 
Fall protection 
Falls are one of the leading causes of accidents and death on construction sites in Denmark 
as already mentioned. Therefor some measures are already in place on most construction 
==PAGE:16==
Page 15 of 246 
 
sites to prevent these falling accidents. Railings, safety belts, proper securing of ladders, and 
scaffolding are already being utilized in order to prevent falls [19]. 
Safety measures for scaffolding include correct installation, regular inspections and the use 
of handrails and toe boards. 
 
Electrical safety 
Electrical hazards are a significant danger on construction sites in Denmark. Some of the 
electrical safety precautions in place include grounding of electrical equipment, ground fault 
circuit breaker and correct usage and storage of extension cords. Workers also receive 
relevant training for working near electrical hazards. 
 
Fire fighting 
Existing firefighting measures include having fire extinguishers on the construction sites, 
regular fire drills, and ensuring that flammable materials are being stored correctly. The 
workers also receive training in how to behave and react in the event of a fire [21]. 
 
Current state of technology in construction safety 
Technologies for construction site safety have been gaining increasing interest over the 
years since the use of basic safety equipment like PPE started [3] to improve health and 
safety conditions for workers.  
There are a lot of studies regarding the use of advanced technologies like BIM, 4DCAD, VR, 
GIS, drones, robotics, automation, sensing and warning technologies. These tools offer 
innovative methods for data collection, monitoring, training, and planning which all 
contributes to improved safety practices and the prevention of accidents in construction 
projects [22]. 
 
Overview of sensors 
Location based sensors 
In this study the focus will remain on the use of location-based technologies for monitoring 
and improving safety on construction sites. In this chapter a short explanation of some of 
the existing location-based technologies will be given with some context of their benefits 
and limitations in the construction industry.  
 
Global Positioning System (GPS) 
Global Positioning System (GPS) is a system that provides fast and accurate 3D coordinates 
for points, lines and planes through satellites, ground control stations and receivers. It has 
gained a significant role in construction safety management in monitoring resources like 
==PAGE:17==
Page 16 of 246 
 
machinery, workers, and construction equipment. GPS technology is an effective tool for 
tracking movement of objects in an outdoor environment, but it has some limitations when 
used indoors or in places with obstacles like tunnels and basements [23]. In such areas the 
performance of the GPS can be degraded, with reduced accuracy and reliable positioning 
due to block of signals. Field tests conducted in Hong Kong shed light on the limitations of 
relying solely on GPS for building project management in highly dense urban areas. The 
results show that when tracking a concrete mixer-truck in highly dense urban areas the 
accuracy error was up to 10 m when combining GPS, Bluetooth, and dead reckoning vehicle 
navigation [24]. An example of a GPS tracker can be seen below. 
Figure 4: Example of a GPS tracker [25] 
 
Figure 5: Example of size comparison [26] 
 
Radio Frequency Identification (RFID) 
Radio Frequency Identification (RFID) technology uses radio frequency waves to identify 
objects by retrieving digital data stored in RFID tags. The RFID system is made of a reader 
that transmits radio waves, and a tag that consists of a microchip with an antenna [27]. 
The RFID tags have a unique serial number, and the tags are available in two forms: Active 
and Passive RFID tags. The active tags have their own power source while the passive tags 
are without a power source, which has benefits in terms of the price and size. RFID 
technology performs well in both outdoor and indoor environments where satellite signals 
are unavailable [28]. 
There are several studies and experiments with RFID technology in the construction industry 
and other industries like healthcare, manufacturing, and food industries. Some examples in 
construction includes real time monitoring of construction assets like resources, machinery, 
workers, and construction equipment [29].  
A study implemented RFID technology in an underground construction site in China to 
investigate and recognize risky behaviors of workers to prevent accidents [30]. While 
another study used RFID technology as method for proximity detection and alerting systems 
==PAGE:18==
Page 17 of 246 
 
as a proactive way to prevent accidents [31]. Field experiments were carried out to simulate 
real-life interactions between workers and construction equipment. The results 
demonstrate that proximity detection and alert technologies can effectively provide alerts 
to equipment operators at predetermined ranges. 
RFID technology is recognized as the most trending sensing technologies in the field of real 
time location sensing. Although it still has a slow adaptation in construction, due to some 
limitations with range issues and problems with simultaneous identifications of multiple 
tags [29]. Examples of both an active and a passive RFID can be seen below. 
 
Figure 6: Example of passive RFID [32] 
 
Figure 7: Example of active RFID [33] 
 
Ultra-Wideband (UWB) 
Ultra-Wideband technology is a short-range wireless radio technology that can 
communicate with high bandwidth to transfer information, while also being energy 
efficient. Ultra-Wideband technology is a useful tool for real time location tracking with its 
ability to give highly accurate location estimations, using various methods like Received 
Signal Strength (RSS), Time of Arrival (ToA), Time Difference of Arrival (TDoA), and Angle of 
Arrival (AoA). The AoA-based ranging technique, which uses direction-sensitive antennas, is 
commonly used in UWB-based RTL systems [34]. 
A study finds Ultra-Wideband technology beneficial for construction safety management, by 
monitoring and analyzing the data of real time locations of construction resources provided 
by the UWB [35]. The movement speed of workers, objects and trajectories can be useful 
for the planning of safe pathways. Ultra-Wideband technology is well suited for construction 
project that involves interior spaces and harsh environments where there are wooden 
materials blocking the signals, since its wide bandwidth allows it to transmit signals through 
obstructions, ensuring reliable and accurate positioning data in indoor construction 
environments [36] [37] [38]. However, there are some limitations when considering 
obstructing materials and layout of the site. Results from another study show that metal 
enclosed surfaces cause performance issues of location estimation with an average error of 
about 50 cm [39]. The performance of UBW is well when line of sight is maintained, but the 
precision deteriorates with increased blockages. Understanding these factors is crucial for 
optimizing UWB use in construction. An example of a UWB-tracker can be seen below. 
==PAGE:19==
Page 18 of 246 
 
 
Figure 8: Example of a UWB tracker [40]. 
 
Zigbee 
Zigbee is a wireless communication technology for short ranges with low transmission speed 
and low energy consumption that is mostly used for data transmission between electronic 
devices. Contrary to UWB, ZigBee uses a narrow bandwidth of 2 MHz.  UWB based 
technology offers high accuracy but is complex and power consuming, making it 
complicated because of the high cost of wireless sensor networks. Alternatively narrow 
bandwidth positioning methods like Zigbee has gained attention [41]. Many studies have 
explored Zigbee’s potential for distance measurements [42] [43].  Zigbee technology has its 
limitation in terms of bandwidth usage, power consumption and compatibility with 
standards and regulations like IEEE 802.15.4 [44]. 
 
Real Time kinematics Global Positioning System (RTK-GNSS) 
Real Time Kinematics, in short RTK, is a technique used to increase the accuracy of Global 
Navigation Satellite System (GNSS).  It is a centimeter level precise positioning technique 
that can be used for navigation, surveying applications and for construction safety 
management, by monitoring the real time location on construction resources. 
GNSS refers to a constellation of satellites that provide accurate 3D coordinates for 
positioning through satellites, ground control stations and receivers. The most well-known 
GNSS system is the Global Positioning System (GPS) as mentioned in a previous section.   
RTK increases the accuracy of GNSS positioning by using a fixed base station that is used as a 
reference point which sends out correctional data to a moving receiver (rover station). 
Therefore, the system requires a base station and a minimum of 1 rover station receiver. 
The base station receives signals from the GNSS satellites and calculates the precise 
positioning coordinates. The rover receiver, which can be mobile or stationary, receives 
signals from the same satellites and compares them with the base station's measurements 
for corrections in real-time. How the RTK works can be seen on the figure below. It is worth 
noting that RTK-GNSS prototypes today are much smaller in size than shown on the figure. 
==PAGE:20==
Page 19 of 246 
 
 
Figure 9: Overview of how high accuracy is achieved using RTK [45]. 
The rover uses the carrier phase measurements to estimate typical GPS errors. By 
differencing the carrier phase measurements between the base station and the rover, the 
rover can determine its precise position with centimeter-level accuracy in real-time [45]. 
Below is an example of the prototype RTK-GNSS system. The board, which does the RTK 
corrections, is connected to a satellite receiver and an antenna. The receiver receives signals 
from the satellites while the antenna allows communication between base station and rover 
stations. 
 
Figure 10: Example of the prototype RTK-GNSS system. One set is used as a base station while the other is used as a rover 
station [46]. 
==PAGE:21==
Page 20 of 246 
 
Methods 
Overview 
In order to gather information and tackle the problem description at hand, a set of different 
methods have been utilized. Extensive research about the current state of safety on 
construction sites and the state of the utilization of advanced technology on construction 
sites has been conducted, to investigate areas that need further investigation.  
A special topics course, “Advanced technologies in construction” taught by Professor Jochen 
Teizer, was participated in in parallel to the thesis process, as the course was deemed highly 
relevant to the thesis, and a lot of useful knowledge could be gained in this course.  
As another part of the investigation process, interviews were conducted with several 
companies from Denmark, that are involved in the construction industry one way or 
another. These were conducted in order to collect fresh and new data that could be 
representative of the construction industry in Denmark. 
Furthermore, a kind of prototype was developed in the form of algorithms that are relevant 
to a potential solution, and that could help demonstrate the solution in practice. 
All the aforementioned methods will be explained in detail and how exactly they were 
utilized and planned, in the sections below. 
 
Research 
Extensive research about the current safety practices of the construction industry around 
the world and Denmark was carried out. Supplemented with some statistics of injuries and 
fatalities in Denmark and the current the state of sensor technology in construction. The 
gathered knowledge on the above-mentioned subjects are from articles, case studies and 
reports. In order to find relevant articles, google scholar has been the primary search 
engine.  Where search words like construction site safety, sensor technologies in 
construction safety, accidents in construction sites and accident types provided a lot of 
relevant information about the state of the art and background in this thesis.   
 
Parallel course 
The special course “Advanced technologies in construction” is a 5 ECTS course and is taught 
by Professor Jochen Teizer. Professor Jochen Teizer has himself done a lot of research and 
articles on the topic of advanced technology in construction and construction safety. 
The course teaches about the different types of advanced technologies that exist, and how 
they are being used, or could be used, on construction sites globally. Examples of these 
include point clouds, 3D-scanning, 3D-printing, real time location sensing, wearable 
technologies and RTK-GNSS systems. 
The course was participated in alongside, and at the same time as, this thesis was being 
conducted. This allowed us to draw inspiration and knowledge from the special topics 
==PAGE:22==
Page 21 of 246 
 
course, that could then be further investigated in depth within the scope of Danish 
construction sites, in this thesis. 
 
Interviews 
Purpose 
The purpose of the interviews is to analyze how the different relevant parties in the 
construction industry in Denmark react to the prospect of implementing advanced 
technologies on the construction sites. The purpose of the interviews is to highlight 
differences in opinions and investigate what kind of companies think the implementation 
will be a good idea. The interviews should also give an idea of the complications that could 
occur, seen from the companies’ perspective, that can then be used to theoretically improve 
upon the implantation process in order to overcome these obstacles. 
 
Participants 
A wide selection of relevant companies have been interviewed in Denmark, in order to get 
responses on the implementation of advanced technologies in construction sites, and this is 
done to have as many points of views as possible. The participants were chosen because 
they are directly relevant and involved on the construction sites or represent people that 
are directly involved in the construction sites. The involved participants are a collection of 
project owners, contractors, construction vehicle rental companies and unions.  
A total of 11 participants were interviewed. 2 of these were project owners, 4 of them were 
contractors, 2 of them were construction vehicle rental companies and 3 of them were 
unions. All the participants will be introduced shortly below. 
 
Hovedstadens Letbane (Project owner) 
Hovedstadens Letbane are project owners for both the Metro and the currently-in-
construction Light Rail in Denmark. Hovedstadens Letbane opened their first metro 
construction back in the 19th of October 2002, and the metro has since then been a major 
part of the infrastructure in Copenhagen. The Metro is an unmanned light train, and in the 
29th of December 2019, the Metro structure was expanded with Cityringen [47]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane are now also owners of the upcoming Greater Copenhagen Light Rail 
project, which spans a distance of 28 km from Ishøj to Lyngby and will have 29 stations. The 
planning of this project started back in 2011, while the construction process started in 2018. 
The project is currently in construction and is expected to be finished in 2025 [48]. 
 
Campus Service (Project owner) 
Campus Service are project owners, with around 200 members, and are responsible for 
every project and building being built or renovated on the grounds of Technical University of 
==PAGE:23==
Page 22 of 246 
 
Denmark (DTU), which are located in Lyngby, Ballerup and Risø in Denmark and in 
Greenland [49]. 
 
Arkil (Contractor) 
Arkil is a big contractor firm that deals with anything related to construction underground 
which involves foundations, tunnelling and sewer works. They also extend and maintain 
roads and construct ports and bridges [50]. 
Arkil was founded in 1941 and they currently have 2.000 employees and a revenue of 3.7 
billion DKK [50]. Arkil have their own subcontractors, so everything they do is in-house [51]. 
 
Enemærke & Petersen (Contractor) 
Enemærke & Petersen is another contractor firm that deals with renovation of general 
housing, schools, and institutions. They also deal with new constructions of public 
institutions and business constructions among other things [52].  
Enemærke & Petersen was founded in 1975 where it started as a roofing company [53]. 
Enemærke & Petersen currently have 700 employees and 300 employees in their 2 
subsidiary companies. They have a revenue of 2.3 billion DKK. If also including their 
subsidiary companies, they have a revenue of 3.3 billion DKK. Enemærke & Petersen have 
50 active construction sites on average each year [54]. 
 
NCC (Contractor) 
NCC is a Swedish-owned contractor firm, that deals with a wide range of areas. These 
include new construction and renovation of everything from public housing and business 
constructions to larger constructions like office buildings and hospitals. They also deal with 
asphalting, roads, sewer works, and water supply installations [55].  
NCC is divided into 4 areas: NCC Building Nordic, NCC Infrastructure, NCC Industry and NCC 
Property Development A/S [56]. 
As of 2021, NCC has 13.000 employees worldwide, whereas 2.000 of these are situated in 
Denmark. NCC’s worldwide revenue is 53 billion SEK per year, and when looking specifically 
in Denmark, the revenue is 6.6 billion DKK per year [56]. 
 
Tscherning (Contractor) 
Tscherning is a contractor firm that primarily deals with demolition and larger construction 
projects where they deal with heating, water, drains, sewage work and any other 
underground-related work, before the main construction phase begins [57]. 
Per 30th of June 2022, Tscherning has 240 employees and a revenue of 16 billion DKK per 
year [58]. 
==PAGE:24==
Page 23 of 246 
 
3F (Union) 
3F is a worker union, that aims to develop and improve work life for their members. One of 
3F’s departments, 3F Byggegruppen, specializes in the construction industry and represent 
diamond cutters, concrete workers, earth workers, bricklayers, demolition workers, 
structural engineers, machine operators, asphalt workers, roofing workers, stonemasons 
and carpenters among other things. As per March 2023, 3F had 262.339 members, whereas 
57.846 are members of the 3F Byggegruppen department [59]. 
 
Bygherreforeningen (Union) 
Bygherreforeningen is a union for project owners, that takes care of their members’ 
interests, both in relation to the law but also in relation to their cooperation with other 
players in the industry, in order to create skill development opportunities. They guide their 
members on what it means to have the professional role as a project owner. They also offer 
a variety of courses where their members can become wiser on different topics such as the 
law, economy, sustainability, and digitalization, among other things [60]. 
 
Dansk Industri (Union) 
Dansk Industri is Denmarks’ biggest employer and business organization. They represent 
19.500 companies both small and big, across all industries in Denmark. They take care of the 
members’ employer-interests and political interests, both in Denmark but also abroad. 
Dansk Industri has 800 employees [61]. 
 
GSV (Rental company) 
GSV is a big rental company, that rent out construction equipment to contractor companies. 
The companies they primarily rent out to are some of the big contractor companies in 
Denmark like Per Aarsleff and Barslund among others. They rent out everything from small 
hand tools to bigger machines such as excavators. They call themselves a “one-stop shop”, 
which means that what they cannot provide they rent, so that they may rent it out to their 
customers. They have a close relationship with some of the producers of these machines, so 
GSV can affect the production if the need arises [62]. 
GSV was founded in 1964, and currently has 580 employees, distributed over 24 
departments in Denmark [63]. 
 
Loxam (Rental Company) 
Loxam is another rental company, that rent out construction equipment to construction 
sites. They also rent out everything from smaller machines and lifts to bigger machines like 
excavators and dump trucks [64]. 
Loxam has 11.800 employees worldwide, distributed over 1.091 departments. In Denmark 
Loxam has 260 employees distributed over 28 departments [65]. 
==PAGE:25==
Page 24 of 246 
 
Interview questions 
During the interviews, the majority of the questions were asked to every single participant. 
Some exceptions occurred, where the questions were not relevant for that type of 
company, or were the participant already answered a question in advance. But overall, all 
the contractor companies were asked the same questions, all the project owners were 
asked the same questions, and so on. This was very important in order to be able to 
compare the answers across the companies later on.  
A set of questions was also prepared to be used for interviews with the workers themselves 
from the construction sites, but these were never used, as the contractor companies that 
were asked to set this up, never came back to us.  
The prepared questions used in the interviews are translated into English and are listed 
below. 
 
Questions for project owners: 
• Can you tell us a little bit about who you guys are, and what you do? What types of 
projects are you guys involved in? 
• Do you think that’s accidents on construction sites happen often, and is still a major 
problem? 
• What types of accidents do you experience, or have you heard of, happens most on 
construction sites? 
• Do you think that accidents where vehicles or cranes are involved, is something that 
happens often, and is a major problem? 
• Do you guys do anything from your side to prevent accidents and improve the safety 
on construction sites? 
- If yes, what do you do? 
• How much do you prioritize safety on construction sites in relation to process and 
economy? 
• What are your opinions on the implementation of advanced technology, such as 
location-based GPS sensors and cameras worn by workers and installed in vehicles, 
as a tool to improve the safety on construction sites and to prevent of accidents? 
• What do you think your customers’ opinions on this would be? 
• Do you think that the implementation of this technology realistically could improve 
the safety on construction sites? 
• Do you think that the implementation of this technology will be worth it in spite of 
the expenses that could occur with the installation and maintenance of these 
technologies? 
• Which complications do you think could occur with the implementation of this 
advanced technology? 
• Can this complication be overcome? 
- if yes, how so?

Interview procedure 
As all the interviewees and authors of this report were Danish speaking, the interviews were 
conducted in Danish. 
The interviews were prepared in advance, where the questions asked depended on what 
kind of interviewee was interviewed, as can be seen on the list of questions in the section 
above. The interviewers were dynamically tracking which questions were being answered 
throughout the interviews. This means, that when an interviewee already answered another 
question when answering an asked question, that question was not repeated to the 
interviewees, unless a clarification was needed, or the answer was not deemed fulfilling 
enough.  
All interviewees were asked if it was okay with them to record the interview. With no 
objections from any of the participants, all interviews were recorded from start to end. 
==PAGE:29==
Page 28 of 246 
 
The interviews were initially aimed to be conducted via Microsoft Teams. This would make 
transcribing the interviews easier, as Microsoft Teams has a built-in transcription tool. 
Although this was not possible for 6 of the interviews.  Instead in these cases, the recordings 
from these interviews were taken through Microsoft Word’s transcription algorithm. 
 
Data processing 
The data collected from the interviews will be analyzed and discussed in the results and 
discussion sections. Here the answers from the different participants will be compared and 
processed in order to arrive at a conclusion of what the different companies in the 
construction industry in Denmark think are the major problems in regard to accidents and 
construction safety, what they think of the implementation of advanced technology on 
construction sites, and whether they think that it will improve the safety on the construction 
sites. The answers from the interviews will be organized and sorted by the questions asked 
as a main factor, to make comparisons easier and make it easier to read and understand for 
the readers. 
As mentioned in the section above, all the interviews have been transcribed by using 
transcription algorithms. The accuracy of this method is not as accurate as manual 
transcription due to the nature of spoken everyday language being little different than 
written language, but it saves a lot of time. The transcriptions allow for easier processing 
when working with the answers from the interviews, and in cases where the transcription is 
unclear, the recordings were looked at for clarification. 
 
Limitations 
As with all things, this method presents some complications and limitations. These 
limitations that occur as a result of the chosen method will be mentioned in this section. 
 
The quantity of interviewees 
This limitation is a result of having too few participants. Even though a lot of potential 
targets for the interviews were initially contacted, only a few of them actually responded 
and ended up being interviewed. 11 participants to be specific. 
In an industry as large as the construction industry in Denmark, 11 participants may not be 
representative enough for a generalization of the industry. Especially not when these 11 
participants have different roles in the construction industry. 2 of them were project 
owners, 4 of them were contractors, 2 of them were construction vehicle rental companies 
and 3 of them were unions. 
Due to this, the 11 participants’ representation of the construction industry may or may not 
be accurate and representative enough. 
  
==PAGE:30==
Page 29 of 246 
 
The participants’ occupation 
Another limitation that could be an issue and warrants some consideration is the occupation 
of the different participants. It is important to note that most of these have an occupation 
of something that directly has to do with work environment or construction safety. The fact 
that it is their job to promote safety and safe work environments and prioritize safety over 
other aspects such as economy and time schedules, could be an issue as it may lead to 
biased answers.  
One way to address this issue, is by having additional participants with different job titles 
like company managers, construction managers, foremen and other personnel involved in 
construction projects. This could help offset the issue by generating results from different 
and a broader range of perspectives. 
 
Lack of answers from workers from the construction sites 
Another limitation is the lack of answers from workers who work directly on the 
construction sites like craftsmen and artisans. These people would have been crucial to 
include as participants, since the implementation of advanced technology directly affects 
them, as they are the ones who will be wearing this technology and be monitored. But due 
to reasons already explained in the “Interview questions” section, this was not possible. 
 
Development of prototype algorithms 
Purpose 
The purpose of developing these algorithms that can process the data from the RTK-GNSS 
systems, is to make it more efficient, fast, and easy for users to use and understand the 
outputted data from the RTK-GNSS systems. 
The raw data that the RTK-GNSS produces may be incomprehensible for a lot of people in its 
raw form and would require additional manual data processing to be useful. The purpose of 
the algorithms is to translate and automatically process this data into something more 
understandable, while being able to input some pre-specified parameters, according to 
what is relevant for the user and what results the user is looking for, through a user-friendly 
user interface. The algorithm will also be able to visualize the findings, all to make it easier 
for the users to understand. 
This tool will act as an after-the-fact tool which will help companies analyze the findings and 
recordings from the RTK-GNSS systems and help them determine what kind of actions can 
be conducted in order to improve upon the safety on the construction sites. 
The algorithms will also be made into standalone executable files, so that any person can 
run the algorithm just from having the single file, without the need to have all the necessary 
modules and packages preinstalled on the computer. 
 
==PAGE:31==
Page 30 of 246 
 
Programming tools 
The main software used to develop the algorithms is Visual Studio Code and the language 
used is Python. These choices were made as it was the only familiar tools at our disposal. A 
lot of packages and modules have been used for the algorithms. These include pandas, 
pyproj, scipy, matplotlib, gmplot, math, numpy, tkinter, io, sys, and shapely. 
With no experience in coding and programming, a lot of the coding was only made possible 
by self-learning, searching online and by asking Kepeng Hong, a fellow DTU student, a 
couple of times when needed. 
 
Development of the algorithms 
In the case of this thesis, 2 algorithms were developed and made into standalone programs. 
The first algorithm was developed to be able to process RTK-GNSS data and investigate and 
detect every time a worker was within a pre-defined distance of a construction vehicle. 
The second algorithm was developed to be able to process the same RTK-GNSS data and 
detect whenever a worker entered a pre-defined danger zone and display the trajectory the 
worker had in that situation. 
The function of both algorithms and how they work in detail, is explained in the results 
section. 
 
Testing of the algorithms 
A single instance of RTK-GNSS data was used as a basis to develop the two algorithms. The 
data from the RTK-GNSS was recorded for a rail project in Germany. This data set was 
therefore naturally also used as a testing method for the algorithms. 
In the case of the first algorithm, the testing was simple since it mainly included proximity 
distances, which could be confirmed by looking at the visual results. 
In the case of the second algorithm, the same data set was used, but additionally the 
algorithm needs a set of coordinates to setup up the pre-defined danger zone. These 
additional coordinates were randomly selected within the location and extent of the data 
set via Google Maps. 
Due to limited resources, it was not possible to generate our own data set, which would 
have made it possible to do manual comparisons in regard to the accuracy of the results. 
 
Limitations 
This method and the creation of the algorithms also allow some limitations to present 
themselves. This section will mention these limitations and what the consequences of these 
limitations can be. 
==PAGE:32==
Page 31 of 246 
 
No availability of self-made data sets 
The lack of the possibility of recording our own data with the RTK-GNSS system presents a 
question about accuracy. Since it was not possible to be on-site when the data set was 
recorded, it was not possible to do manual comparison and verify the accuracy of the RTK-
GNSS measurements. Although, based on our general knowledge about the RTK-GNSS 
system, it is assumed that the data that was used during this thesis is very accurate down to 
a centimeter level accuracy. 
 
No availability of multiple data sets 
The lack of multiple and different data sets presents another limitation. Since the algorithms 
were based and built around one data set, it was impossible to tailor the algorithms to be 
able to handle different data sets and even if it was attempted, there was no way to verify 
this aspect with absolute certainty. Therefor the resulting algorithms are as of this moment, 
not universal, even though they were intended to be in the future. They can only be used 
with the specific data set that was available to us. 
 
Limited coding knowledge 
Due to limited programming experience, the used programming was all self-taught. This has 
resulted in limitations because of uncertainty about which modules and packages exist and 
what was even possible or not to achieve through programming. Based on the limitation of 
not knowing what different packages and modules exist, a problem occurred, where the 
module that was used for the Google Maps satellite view only allowed zooming in to a 
specific limit. This can sometimes make the results of the second algorithm hard to visually 
read and comprehend, especially when trying to distinguish the plotted trajectory. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:33==
Page 32 of 246 
 
Results 
Overview 
Based on the mentioned used methods, the investigation has yielded several results. These 
results appear in the form of the answers gotten during the interviews and the resulting 
standalone prototype programs derived from the developed algorithms.  
The aforementioned results will be shown and presented in detail in the sections below, 
where the results from the interviews will firstly be sorted by company type and secondly by 
the questions asked. The raw transcriptions for the interviews can be seen on appendices 1 
through 11. The recordings of the interviews can be seen on appendices 12 through 21. 
 
Interviews 
Project owners 
Do you think that’s accidents on construction sites happen often, and is still a major 
problem? 
Campus Service (CAS) did not right away have a clear answer. This is because that accidents 
are only registered and reported as accidents if it has resulted in absence from work. 
Otherwise, the incident gets labelled as a close call incident. Furthermore, only accidents 
that has resulted in absence from work are required to be registered and reported to the 
Danish Working Environment Authority. Even though the close call incidents could have 
been very fatal and dangerous, more than some of the actual reported accidents, these are 
not required to be reported, even though a lot of them are happening on construction sites 
[49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane, have experienced a lot of accidents on their construction sites, 
where one of the main factors for these are the limited space on which they operate. Most 
of their construction sites and out in the public, in heavy traffic, which have made their 
construction sites very small and limited in space. The number of accidents they have 
experienced has gone down over the years, but they believe that it is still an actual problem, 
that needs to be dealt with [66]. 
 
What types of accidents do you experience, or have you heard of, happen most on 
construction sites? 
According to Campus Service, the most often occurring accidents are related to cut injuries 
and twist injuries, whereas the most often occurring close calls are related to people not 
respecting off-limit areas, wandering into dangerous zones and crane lifting over people 
[49]. 
According to Hovedstadens Letbane, the most frequent accidents and close calls they 
experience are falls, stumbling, slipping, falling on stairs, overload injuries, falling objects, 
collision accidents between dump trucks, and overturned vehicles [66]. 
 
==PAGE:34==
Page 33 of 246 
 
Do you think that accidents where vehicles or cranes are involved, is something that happens 
often, and is a major problem? 
According to Campus Service, accidents and close calls where vehicles are involved are not 
something that often happens on the construction sites that they are involved in [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane had already answered this question, where the answer was yes, they 
have a lot of accidents and close calls where vehicles are involved [66]. 
 
Do you guys do anything from your side to prevent accidents and improve the safety on 
construction sites?  
- If yes, what do you do? 
Campus Service have a safety coordinator on each of their construction sites, whom they 
have chosen. There is close cooperation between the safety manager (one of the 
interviewees), safety coordinator and the project management in relation to planning the 
construction site. They also host safety inspections on the construction sites every week and 
are very involved in the ongoings on their construction sites. All in all, Campus Service does 
a lot of work to ensure safety on their construction sites. They do everything by the book, 
plus a little more [49]. 
According to Hovedstadens Letbane, they have different measure in place to deal with 
safety on construction sites. They offer a course for their contractors, more specifically, the 
work environment coordinators, where they among other things use an analysis form called 
Tripod Beta, that is about not just looking at the cause of incidents but also digging deeper 
and analysing the factors leading up to the incident. They regularly conduct these tripod 
analyses in collaboration with their contractors, where they have a dialogue about different 
risks associated with the project. Another issue is that the interviewee does not believe that 
they report enough of the close call incidents that happen [66]. 
 
How much do you prioritize safety on construction sites in relation to process and economy?  
This question was not asked to Campus Service, as they had already more or less answered 
this question, during the other answers [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane admits that it is a kind of dilemma. They prioritize safety very high 
but there must be a balance. Economy and time schedules also play a vital for a successful 
project. So, there must be a balance between prioritization of economy and time schedules 
and the work environment. But if they were in a situation where they had to prioritize, they 
would prioritize the safety first and foremost at any given time [66]. 
 
==PAGE:35==
Page 34 of 246 
 
What are your opinions on the implementation of advanced technology, such as location-
based GPS sensors and cameras worn by workers and installed in vehicles, as a tool to 
improve the safety on construction sites and to prevent of accidents? 
Campus Service thinks that everything that can help improve the safety on construction 
sites is good, but it must not be an inconvenience and it must not be too pricey to 
implement and maintain. They believe that it would be able to produce some very useful 
data, if its easy to use and carry. According to Campus Service, people who would complain 
about being monitored will always be a thing. Those are feelings and Campus Service do not 
care about this, as they think that the concept would give some positive results. They took 
the airport as an example: The airport has a lot of surveillance, monitoring, and body 
searches, and that’s just how it is, and if you do not like working there, you can just find 
another place to work. All in all, they feel very positive about the implementation of this 
concept [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane believes that all technologies that can improve the safety on their 
construction sites is something that is worth looking into and implementing, and they can 
see the big potential that implementation of advanced technologies can provide. It would 
specifically help with their problem of registering and reporting close call incidents, as this 
process would no longer be dependent on the individual person but would be supported by 
hard data from the technologies. The interviewee admits that it could present some risks, 
which consists of the possibility of the workers turning off the technology or ignoring it. All 
in all, they feel very positive about the implementation of technologies on the construction 
sites and believe that it could have a huge positive effect on work environment [66]. 
 
What do you think your customers’ opinions on this would be? 
According to Campus Service, the implementation of the concept should start with the 
project owner. They believe that it is the project owner who should set the requirement 
regarding the implementation of the concept and make it requirement for contractors 
looking to bid in on the project. They believe that the implementation has a higher chance 
of being a success if it comes from the project owner, as opposed to a contractor. Another 
important factor is how one presents the idea. If the cause and potential advantages like 
improvement of safety and prevention of accidents are explained in detail, they believe that 
the involved people will also be more receptive to the idea [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane admits that with implementing this concept, questions could be 
asked about monitoring. But if the monitoring does not affect the private life, and that the 
monitoring is done for the workers’ own safety, he believes that it should not be a problem. 
He believes that there are some situations where it would actually be an advantage to know 
the workers’ positions, for example during an evacuation in an underground tunnel project. 
The interviewee also points out that we have a lot of other requirements on the 
construction sites already, so it would just be a question about getting used to it. All in all, 
the interviewee does not believe that the implementation would come without any 
complications but believes that these can be solved and eventually the concept could be 
implemented [66]. 
==PAGE:36==
Page 35 of 246 
 
Do you think that the implementation of this technology realistically could improve the 
safety on construction sites? 
Campus Service, as already answered in the previous question, believe that the 
implementation of the technology could realistically improve the safety on construction 
sites by producing useful data that can then be used to take further actions towards 
prevention of close calls and accidents [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane believes that the implementation of the concept definitely would 
improve the safety on construction sites, but it cannot exist alone. It requires that people 
still need to be aware of potential risks and hazard. The concept could easily implant a false 
sense of safety and could lead to the workers seeing it as an excuse to no longer be on alert 
for potential risks and hazards. But if it is used as an additional to the already existing 
measures and norms, the interviewee believes that it realistically could improve the safety 
on construction sites [66]. 
 
Do you think that the implementation of this technology will be worth it in spite of the 
expenses that could occur with the installation and maintenance of these technologies? 
This question has also already been answered by Campus Service. They believe that it is 
worth it, but it must not be very pricey nor a nuisance for the affected workers [49]. 
This question has already been answered by Hovedstadens Letbane. They believe that there 
should be a balance between safety measures and economy and time schedules [66]. 
 
Which complications do you think could occur with the implementation of this advanced 
technology? 
According to Campus Service, the most prominent potential complication would be that the 
physical setup would become troublesome and inconvenient. It must not be a nuisance to 
wear this technology for the workers during the work shift. They feel that the mental aspect 
of feeling monitored can easily be tackled. The economy could also be a complication. As 
mentioned earlier, it must not be too pricey to implement [49]. 
This question has already been answered by Hovedstadens Letbane. They believe that some 
complication could occur like the workers feeling monitored, and that the concept could 
implant a false sense of safety, which could lead the workers to lower their guards towards 
potential risks and hazards. 
 
Can this complication be overcome?  
- if yes, how so? 
This question was not asked to Campus Service as it was already more or less answered in 
the previous question [49]. 
This question was not asked to Hovedstadens Letbane either since it was already answered 
in the previous questions [66]. 
==PAGE:37==
Page 36 of 246 
 
Do you have non-Danish-speaking workers on the construction sites?  
- if yes, do you ever experience problems with communication with them? 
Campus Service does have non-Danish-speaking workers on their construction sites. Campus 
Service has set up a requirement that require at least a foreman that can speak English, 
which they think has worked fine. They have not had any problems with communication 
[49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane does have foreign workforces on their projects, but they have 
demanded that the teams should have at least one worker who speaks English. The 
interviewee admits that some information could get lost during the translation and that this 
could be improved upon but does not believe that it is a major issue [66]. 
 
Do you guys have any consequences set up from your side for when workers fail to comply 
with the safety rules on the construction sites?  
Campus Service has not really had the need to issue out consequences. If the need occurs, 
they will issue out a written warning, and if the rule breaking has been repeated, it will lead 
to expulsion. Another possible course of action would be to let the corresponding company 
know and let them take care of it [49]. 
Hovedstadens Letbane have some measures set up where they can go in an interrupt the 
work if the need arises. They also utilize the classic method with 2 warnings and then an 
expulsion the third time, but overall, the consequences are primarily maintained by the 
contractors [66]. 
 
Contractors 
Do you think that’s accidents on construction sites happen often, and is still a major 
problem? 
Arkil does experience accidents very often on construction sites. They believe that they are 
in a good position compared to statistics from other companies, and believe that their 
accidents primarily concern new workers, that have been employed for under 2 years. Half 
of these new workers get into an accident at one point or another [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen also believe that they are in a good position statistically, but also 
believe that they should still do everything they can to keep that good statistic [54]. 
NCC acknowledges that accidents on construction sites are still a major problem because of 
the nature of the industry with dynamic working conditions that may be potentially 
dangerous. But they also believe that they are doing a good job at inspecting the sites for 
mitigation of potential risk of accidents [55]. 
Tscherning frequently has minor work-related accidents and are working towards 
minimizing them, they update their report throughout the year [57]. 
 
==PAGE:38==
Page 37 of 246 
 
What types of accidents do you experience, or have you heard of, happens most on 
construction sites? 
Arkil thinks that it is hard to classify what kind of accidents they experience, due to the 
many kinds of projects they are involved in. But a common denominator is that the workers 
are willing to take risks, which could involve them forgetting their PPE equipment or “just 
have to do this small thing” without taking the time to do that action in a safe way [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen mainly experience accidents that has to do with cutting when using 
cutting equipment, fall from ladders due to lack of proper use and stumbling due to lack of 
clean up [54]. 
NCC mainly experience accidents that involve workers cutting their fingers on sharp objects 
and slipping or falling over objects laying on the floor. They rarely experience fatal accidents 
and have not had one since 2015 [55]. 
Tscherning frequently has minor fall accidents when workers are stepping out of vehicles 
and slipping or twisting their ankles in places where excavating has been conducted in the 
infrastructure department. In demolition projects they mainly experience accidents where 
workers cut themselves [57]. 
 
Do you think that accidents where vehicles or cranes are involved, is something that happens 
often, and is a major problem? 
Arkil does not have accidents where vehicles are involved. The closest thing they have to 
this, are overturned dump trucks [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen do not regularly have accidents where vehicles are involved. They 
had one singular incident with a crane, where it resulted in a worker breaking his toe [54]. 
NCC has recently had two near miss accidents but since no one was harmed they wouldn’t 
classify them as accidents. The first incident involved a crane dropping glass partitions and 
the second incident, a pallet with steel beams collapsed while the crane was collecting the 
beams [55]. 
Tscherning states that they have not had any accidents where vehicles are involved in the 
last 10 years or at least it is not something the interviewed person have any knowledge of 
happening [57]. 
 
Do you guys do anything from your side to prevent accidents and improve the safety on 
construction sites? 
- If yes, what do you do? 
The way Arkil tackles safety on construction sites is primarily by planning. They plan work 
procedures and organize it so it can be executed in a secure and safe manner. They also 
have work descriptions and risk assessments which they go through with their employees 
before every new work process. They also conduct these so-called toolbox meetings once a 
==PAGE:39==
Page 38 of 246 
 
week, where they go over what is going to happen the following week and talk about the 
safety when the need arises [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen also tackle their work environment by planning thoroughly in 
advance. They create work descriptions and conduct meetings in relation to safety on 
construction sites. Furthermore, they conduct regular inspections on their construction sites 
to ensure that the safety on construction sites is maintained. They also investigate close call 
encounters and situations that could have led to accidents, in order to prevent accidents 
from happening in the future. They also investigate the project material and safety, and 
work environment plans before bidding on a project, to ensure that the safety is maintained 
sufficiently [54]. 
NCC provides clear information and instructions on how to perform tasks in an accordance 
with the general safety guidelines. They setup restricted zones and inform workers to be 
aware before entering a dangerous zone without the machine operator’s acknowledgment 
[55]. 
Tscherning also tackles their work environment by planning thoroughly in advance. They 
create work descriptions and conduct meetings in relation to safety on construction sites 
every morning by holding a 15 min safety meeting. So that workers are aware of potential 
hazards in relation to falls or trips/slips on objects. They also provide the machine operators 
with training and instruction [57]. 
 
How much do you prioritize safety on construction sites in relation to process and economy? 
Arkil prioritizes safety very highly. It is their first priority over everything else. Arkil believes 
that there is always something more one can do in relation to safety on the construction 
sites. As soon 1 accident takes place, that means that they have not done their job well 
enough. Which is something they will have in focus in the coming time, which includes the 
workers’ own risk assessments and teach them to better plan their work process every time 
they are about to start something new [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen also prioritizes safety very highly. They have no problem stopping a 
work process immediately, if a potential risk or hazard is identified, even if it ends up costing 
them a lot of money as a consequence (for example when they have a rented vehicle on 
site) [54]. 
NCC also prioritizes safety highly on paper but are not entirely sure if that correlates to 
reality. It is a top priority but at the same time it is also a business, so there should be a 
balance between the cost and benefits [55]. 
Tscherning also prioritizes safety very highly and keeps track of accidents in their records 
[57]. 
 
==PAGE:40==
Page 39 of 246 
 
Do you use your own machines and vehicles, or do you rent them? 
Arkil both own some of their own vehicles but also rent some of vehicles that they do not 
own themselves [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen’s subsidiary firms own some construction vehicles and equipment. 
Otherwise, they primarily rent the vehicles they need such as cranes and loaders [54]. 
NCC primarily rent the vehicles they need such as cranes, excavators, and loaders. But their 
infrastructures department owns machinery since there is a lot of need for them constantly 
in the projects that the department is involved in [55]. 
Tscherning rents most of the equipment e.g., lifts for their demolition departments and for 
their infrastructures department, machinery, and vehicles [57]. 
 
What are your opinions on the implementation of advanced technology, such as location-
based GPS sensors and cameras worn by workers and installed in vehicles, as a tool to 
improve the safety on construction sites and to prevent of accidents? 
Arkil does not believe that the concept is necessary as they cannot see what good it would 
do, or what purpose it would serve. They believe that they should have planned their work 
so that there should be no reason to implement the concept [51]. 
Enemærke & Pedersen admits that they have not considered advanced technology as a 
possibility for improving safety on construction sites. They mainly utilize physical methods 
like signs and cones, to make sure that vehicles do not need to drive backwards, since 
backwards driving are often the cause of vehicle related accidents, according to Enemærke 
& Pedersen [54]. 
NCC believes that the concept of location-based sensor can be a useful tool that can 
improve the safety on their construction sites, but they would like the data tags from 
workers to be anonymous since the do not want to investigate the workers individually but 
rather their overall behavior and tendencies. Like trajectories of workers to better plan safe 
zones and pathways. They do not want the data to be used for enforcing fines or to give the 
individual worker warnings, but more as a tool for information gathering to take proactive 
approaches [55]. 
Tscherning does not believe that the concept is necessary as they cannot see what good it 
would do, or what purpose it would serve. They believe that they have planned their work 
so that there should be no reason to implement the concept since they mostly have the site 
for themselves. They have GPS installed in vehicles and when workers enter the site they 
scan their ID cards, which is enough regarding technology [57]. 
 
What do you think your employees’ and workers’ opinions on this would be? 
Arkil – The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and therefore the 
question was deemed not relevant.

Enemærke & Pedersen can imagine that the workers would not be too happy about this

construction sites. It could make the workers think twice about taking a break, which 
normally would have been acceptable, but now it would beg the question “can I actually be 
allowed to take this break now that I have to be 100% effective all the time, because now I 
am actually being monitored.” [54]. 
NCC thinks that as long as the technology is anonymous and is wearable for workers to have 
on without being a distraction it would not be a problem. However, they do have some 
concerns about the size and battery performance. If workers must charge the equipment 
themselves that may also pose a problem/inconvenience [55]. 
Tscherning - The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and 
therefore the question was deemed not relevant.

Enemærke & Pedersen does not believe that the implementation of advanced technology

present [54]. 
NCC had already answered this in the previous question, they believe the implementation of

safe zones and pathways [55]. 
Tscherning - The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and

Do you think that the implementation of this technology will be worth it in spite of the

Arkil - The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and therefore the

they have considered or formed some thoughts about. 
NCC believes that it would be worth it if the technology ends up improving the safety on 
construction sites. 
Tscherning - The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and 
therefore the question was deemed not relevant.

Arkil – The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and therefore the 
question was deemed not relevant.

workers effectivity and sense of freedom [54]. 
NCC answered that in a previous question, battery and size of the sensors was a major 
concern. They are concerned that if workers must charge the sensors themselves a lot of 
them would not be charged or workers would not wear the sensors. Another issue was

Tscherning - The question was not asked as they were opposed to the concept, and

Can this complication be overcome?
